And I treated a woman for an abscess that was inside her throat, namely one of small size; and it had almost completely closed her throat, so that the woman could not exhale through the passage that was blocked, and she could neither eat nor drink, and she would have died if she had stayed like that for one or two days. And the abscess was so swollen that it had pushed out two pieces through the two nostrils. I therefore acted quickly, and inserted a probe into one of them, then pulled it out and extracted a large amount of pus; then I did the same thing in the other nostril, and opened her mouth and pressed her tongue; after that, I inserted the probe into these abscesses and cut off a part of it, and only a little blood came out; and thus the passage of the woman's throat was unblocked, and she began to drink water and then wine, and I continued to cut into this abscess for a long time; and the abscess grew again and filled the space that I had emptied, so that it became very large; then I pressed on it and cauterized it inside her throat: thus it ceased to grow, and I left. And I do not know what God did with her after I left.
of the grape (from Latin "uvam") or eye (from Latin "oculus"), Old French used "ueil" for both, so "de l'uve" is ambiguous in this language and could mean either "of the grape" or "of the eye".
When pus descends to the eye, and it is swollen and elongated, it is called "perpendicular"; and if it is below thick and round, it is called "eye". When it is treated as we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help, and you see that the chief abscess has calmed down, and it is thin, then it is necessary to make an incision; and the one that is rounded gathered and not long; and it is blood-red or flesh-colored or black, then it is necessary to avoid making an incision in it. Because there is great danger to the patient. Therefore, when you see it, according to its swelling and length, you should have the patient sit in front of the sun, and press their tongue with the instrument that we have mentioned before; then insert the probe into the eye, and pull it down, and make an incision with one of the two instruments that I have said in the incision of the two almonds. And it is necessary to not cut further than the excess that is beyond the natural limit; because if you cut more, it harms the voice, and speech; and after the incision, use what we have said in the incision of the two almonds, and cure it until it is healed. And if the patient is afraid of cutting, then it is necessary to use a trick with cauterization without fear. And the manner of cauterization is lukewarm. And this is that the patient places their head in your lap, and then presses their tongue with the instrument that we have mentioned in the chapter on cautery, and apply live coals, so that they are not too thick nor too thin; and use the movement of this instrument, of which this is the shape:
And have the patient place a hair or put the medicine on it, such as the handles of a spoon, and place the instrument full of medicine on the eye; and let the patient lie on their side, so that saliva and mucus flow from their mouth with the medicine inside, so that it does not go down to the throat and cause choking; then hold your hand over all the medicine and press it on the eye, for half an hour, until you see it turn black and the bites of the medicine are calmed; and if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around some instrument, and moisten the cotton in the medicine, and insert the instrument with the cotton into the wound, as much as the cotton adheres to the eye; and do this often, until the cauterization is complete, then let it dry and fall off after three or five days. And if you need to start the cauterization of the medicine again, then start it again, and after the cauterization wipe around the eye with cotton wrapped in butter, and clean what is around the medicine; and rinse with cold water, and cure with gargling until it heals.
This treatment is safer than incision or cauterization if it is treated with fumigation in this manner: take chamomile and hyssop and oregano and rue and camomile and elder and similar herbs, and gather them all or some of them in one pot, and put them to boil; and let the pot be well glued with strong earth, and in the middle of the pot there should be an instrument like this: And whether it be of silver or bronze, and one of the eyes be pushed into the mouth of the patient until the steam reaches the eye through the canal, until the eye becomes lifeless; then restart it often, until the eye dries up, and take care not to do this at the beginning of the hot abscess, for many things can happen; and do not do it except at the decline of a hot abscess. And if this instrument is not present, take a tube and put inside the head an eggshell, so that the mouth of the patient is not too hot; for the eggshell does not allow the steam to overwhelm the mouth, and this is a good remedy together with a cure.
Often a bone or fish spine, or some other object gets stuck in the throat; therefore, you must pull out what you can see, and press the patient's tongue to the sun with all the instrument, so that you may see the object move; and what is not visible and has lodged in the throat, you must make the patient vomit after the food has been cooked in the stomach; perhaps the object will come out through vomiting; or the patient has swallowed a piece of sponge, or a piece of napkin, or a lettuce root, or a piece of dry bread; and tie a string to the sponge and then swallow it; and when it reaches the place, it will swell up, and then you can pull it out; and if it does not come out as we have said, take an instrument of wood in this form:
And let the instrument be curved at the end so that it may enter more easily into the throat, and hold the head up high; and take care not to touch the epiglottis, so that he does not choke or push the bone in further; and it is better if the patient puts the instrument in himself, so that he can feel where the object is; then insert it all the way until the object comes out.
of the throat sinew
Sometimes the tonsils are medicined, as I have said in the division, by the cure of medicines; and when this does not help at all, then look in the sick person's throat in the sun, after you press their tongue with the instrument that I have told you. And if you see the tonsil, then draw it with a tiny hook, or with delicate tweezers that are suitable for this. And if this is not valuable, then take a boiled leech, and put it in the sick person's throat, then put a hot iron in the movement of the leech. And do this often. And if the patient becomes feverish all day. Then take a full glass of cold water, and open the mouth, and throw it in without swallowing, and move it with their hand often; and as soon as the tonsil feels the water, it will fall out.
And if it does not fall out as we have said, then fumigate the patient with asafetida, with the instrument that I have mentioned in the fumigation of the eye; do this many times, for it will fall out without delay. And the manner of fumigation is as follows: take a pot in which there are hot coals, and cover it with a perforated cloth, and through this hole place the opening of the instrument; then put your fumigation in, and the patient puts the end of the cane in their mouth, and closes their mouth so that the smoke does not escape, but reaches the tonsil; and the tonsil will now fall out, and if it does not fall out, then repeat the fumigation many times; and let the patient suffer thirst, and eat salty and bitter things, and do not drink water; and then it will be necessary for it to come out through this treatment.
And the shape of the instrument with which to treat the tonsil is, when it can be seen, similar to curved pliers in order to enter easily into the throat; and it should resemble a bird's beak, and have roughness like sandpaper, so that what it takes, it cannot release; and see here the shape:
The chapter of abscesses.
Many similarities of abscess are, according to what the memory brings hereafter of one and the other in this book, and they are different in perforation and size; and it is in two ways, one of which is in its own likeness, and those that hold moisture. And the second manner is according to the locations where they are in the body. For the abscesses of the head are different from those of the bottom, and the abscesses of the fleshy part are different from those that come in joints, and each one should have its own judgment in the work.
And there are abscesses that should not be pierced until the boil is dead according to its maturation; and there are such as must be pierced when they have grown, and do not die according to their maturation, such as abscesses that are near joints. For when abscesses come near joints, and one leaves it until it is entirely corrupted, perhaps the ligaments and nerves of the joint have been corrupted, which causes contraction in that member. Or the abscess is near the main member, and if you delay the piercing until it is dead, it will harm the main member; or it is near the bottom, one must pierce it as soon as it is dead; and one should not pierce a grown abscess without the completion of digestion, except for those that are near the bottom, lest it corrupts inside and becomes elongated, causing fistula; and cannot be healed in a long time.
And it is necessary that you know the hour of the piercing of the abscess when it is dead according to its maturation, and this is when the pain has subsided, and the departure of the fever, and the lessening of the pain and redness, and the softening and swelling of the head of the abscess, and other signs; and you must perform the piercing in the lowest part of the abscess, and where it is apparent that it has moved; and let the piercing be long and through the body, and if the abscess is in the hands or feet, or in joints and nerves and arteries, and in all places where there is no cavity.
And in those that swell, the incision should be according to the member. And when the abscess is in fleshy parts, it is better to wait and leave the piercing until it has solidified after the completion of digestion, as we have said. For if you pierce it before this, the body's matter will spread from it and be full of much filth; and perhaps the lips and its depths harden. And some abscesses are pierced according to the width of the body, according to what is necessary for the member. And in small abscesses, make small incisions; and in large abscesses, large incisions, and many splits according to the likeness of the size of the abscess; and there are such abscesses where it is necessary to cut out the core, and cover it; when it has become as a piece of cloth, and is dead, one must do as with boils: and there are such that are cut in three angles, and there are such that are cut like the leaf of myrtle, as abscesses of glands; and there are such where a round incision is made, and other cuts and similar cutting.
Those who have no visible abscess, such as a soft, superficial abscess, should be fully lanced. And when the abscess is large and has gathered great pus, you should not pull out all the pus in that hour, but instead remove some of it; then stop up the abscess until the next day, and remove more again; do this repeatedly, increasing the amount each time, until all the pus is removed. Do the same even if the patient is of weak constitution, such as women, young children, old or decrepit men. For the spirit sometimes leaps when the pus is released, which may accidentally kill the patient. Therefore, proceed with great caution in this matter, and after lancing your abscess, clean the wound, and see if the wound from the abscess is small. If it is a clean cut, apply bandages of linen or old cotton. If it is a large abscess with many holes, place a tent in each one so that they come together. And if you have cut off part of the skin and pulled it up, you should fill it with old linen cloth or shreds of linen fabric without moisture, and bind it for three days; then remove it and dress the wound until it heals.
And if bleeding occurs during your procedure, apply cold water and a damp cloth to the area multiple times. If the bleeding persists, provide medical treatment and use the powders I have mentioned in this book, for gentle application. And if it is winter, and the location of the abscess is very sensitive, you must place tents and damp cloths soaked in warm wine and oil on top; and if it is summer, and the area is fleshy, you should apply damp cloths soaked in water and oil, or in wine and cold oil, for three days as we have said. Then loosen the abscess and clean and dress it, as necessary, until it heals.
And this is what one must understand universally in the treatment of abscesses: I have told you how to proceed with the treatment of abscesses, and herein follows the chapter on the subject.
In Old French, "des soros" means "of sores" or "on sores". It may refer to the treatments or cures for various kinds of sores.
Where small abscesses of the head appear, and they are of a kind that are in veils, such as the hock of a chicken, and there are many similar ones. There are some that are fatty and such where there is moisture like pustules; and there are some that are hard and stony to cut into, and there is no doubt if the artery does not oppose it. And the work of cutting consists of testing with every single instrument called an "entromissoire", of which the form comes after, what it contains. For if it contains moisture, one should pierce it from above the long [one]. And when the moisture has been emptied, one should skin without leaving any of the skin that contains that moisture, for it returns again when one leaves anything; then soak cotton in Egyptian ointment, if present; if not, in water and salt, and apply it to the wound, and leave it until the next day; and those who remain overnight will heal this. And then resume the cotton, as before, on the second day, and on the third, until you are certain that nothing remains overnight; then cure the wound of ointment suitable for healing, until it is healed. And if the abscess holds a hard knot, one should cut it along the long [one]; and throw in your lancets, and skin all around, and apply all your care to draw out the cyst that lies within; and if the artery opposes you, do what I told you after. And similarly do for the abscess if it is stony, and cut and heal as I told you.
And the cutting into a hard or moist abscess is easier, for they contain little blood. And if some of these abscesses on the head or where there is no moisture, and this was one of the abscesses that I cut on an old woman's head; and found the abscess like a hard, rough and white stone, and could not break it; and if anyone had struck it, they would have made as much of a wound as with a stone. In other abscesses coming to the head, except for those that come on children's heads and at the roots of the ears, let the opening be as low down as possible so that pus may easily exit; and treat them as is appropriate.
"Des scrophules" is a medical term used to refer to a type of abscess or inflammatory swelling, usually found in groups, typically on the neck or behind the ears. These swellings are commonly known as "scrofula" or "the king's evil". The term comes from the Greek word "skirphos," which means "tubercular swelling." In medieval medical texts, scrofula was often associated with tuberculosis and thought to be caused by an imbalance of bodily fluids or humors. It was also believed that the touch of a king could cure this condition, hence the name "the king's evil".
"Scrophules" often appear on the neck, under the armpits, and in the groin, and sometimes come in other parts of the body, and they are large and interconnected. And each scrofula is specifically within its own cyst, just like nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have said; and there are many varieties of these scrophules. Some contain moisture, and some are harmful that do not want to obey health. In this one that you see in good condition to touch, and it is similar to the color of the body, and moves from every side and is not aerated by the nerves of the neck or artery or conduit, and is not deep; then you must cut it from above, and skin each part, and pull the two lips of the wound with your lancets if you need them, as we have said in abscesses of the head; and draw little by little, and be careful not to cut the nerve or vein, and let the spatula not be too sharp or cutting, so that the hands do not slip, and do not go too far, so that you do not cut what you should not.
And if you cut a vein or artery, and it does not let you work, put in the wound vitriol powder, or any powders that stop blood and constrict the wound; and leave it until the abscess has calmed down, and the wound has softened and begun to rot. And when the bleeding has stopped, return to your work, until you have completed it; then see with your skilled judgment if there remain any small scrofulous pieces, and cut and clean them. And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, it is not at all appropriate to cut this scrofula at its root, but bind it with a double strong thread and let it fall without harm; then fill the wound with old cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, then bandage it.
And if you cut the entire scrofula, it is necessary to bring together the two lips of the wound and sew it now, so that there is no remaining superfluity due to the size of the scrofula. It is necessary to focus on cutting the root of it and pulling it out, and sewing it as we have said. For those scrofulas that hold superfluity, pierce where it appears necrotic; and pierce it in the same way as the one described before, depending [on its condition], then apply dressings with ointment and similar things, so that it corrupts what remains of the corruption; and when the wound is well cleaned, cure it with an ointment drawing out flesh, until it has healed.
The word "de squinance" in Old French can be translated to "of sciatica" in English. Sciatica refers to pain that affects the back, hip, and outer side of the leg, caused by compression or irritation of the sciatic nerve.

of the swollen throat

And it is of two kinds: natural and accidental; the natural one has no malice (evil intent).

of the nuts (or: of the swellings)

With the new (dislocation) there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains one abscess, which is its own sac and is according to the color of the body, and begins like a blister, and becomes swollen.

of the rupture of the artery
When an artery is ruptured and the skin over it is stuck, it often results in abscesses, and similarly, when a vein is swollen and abscessed.

of abscesses of the nerves
Just as an abscess can occur in the artery and the vein, an abscess can also come to the nerves when they are injured or from excessive exertion and similar things.[STHAPHISIUS, Georgius (1541) Opera medica et philosophica. Basileae.
And this happens more often in the shoulder joints and the hands, and in all places where there are joints, and these are severe abscesses, and their color resembles the color of the skin, and they are in many cases painless;
and when it is strongly constricted, the patient feels pain, and it is not deep in the body, but under the chest;
and it can move in all directions, but goes neither forward nor backward, nor downward or upward, and the one who is in joints should not be covered with iron, for there may be contraction;
And the one who is on the head or forehead, makes the skin suppurate. If the abscess is small, hold it with forceps and cut into its root;
and if it is large, suspend it with a string and flay it, then cut it out and stitch up the wound, and care for it until it heals.
of large, dangling pieces (or wounds) Sometimes there occur in the belly and not elsewhere what is called "funge," which resembles them (worms), and its root is fine, and its head is large; and they are both small and very large.

I weighed them and found that one weighed eighteen ounces, and the other six ounces; and the appearance in the incision is such that you look to see if the patient is moist, and the color of the intestines is white and moist with fine roots.

Then cauterize it so that it does not grow back; then treat the wound and make sure not to cut the artery, which is of a foxy color and poor sensation, with a milky appearance.

when the belly protrudes (is distended)

and if it is due to flatulence, it is easy to touch, and the procedure is such that you should look at whether the appearance is due to blood from an artery or a vein, or flatulence. You must leave the wound alone. Because there is doubt and fear, as I have said in the chapter on abscesses, due to the artery and the vein.

then open the middle of the abscess that is stretched over the tie and insert your thumb index finger, and search for the intestines;[Xref-38257][Xref-49015] if you find it caught by the tie, then loosen it and push the intestines back into the belly;[Xref-38257][Xref-49015][INTERNAL-REF]  [INTERNAL-NOTE] The text uses "liien" to refer to the cord or thread used to bind the abscess, but in this sentence it refers to the tie that holds the intestines. of the chancre
We have said in the division the similarities of chancre and how one should work on it with medicines and how to avoid iron surgery; and we say that chancre worsens in the groin, and great caution is needed in its cure. And the ancients said that when a chancre is in a place where it can be scraped, such as a chancre that is on the breasts, and in the thigh, and in similar places, one must remove it completely when it is at the beginning. And when it is large and old, then one should not approach it, for I cannot heal any of them; nor did anyone ever heal any before me.
And the operation, when it is in a place where one can remove it, as we have said, is that you purge the patient of black bile once or twice; then make them bleed if they have clear repletion, then prepare the patient for the procedure; then cast your leeches on the chancre and pull out all the hairs from around it without leaving any behind. Let the blood flow freely and press well on the area with your hand or some other object. If the bleeding is unfavorable to you because of an incision in the artery or vein, cauterize until the blood has stopped flowing. Then treat the wound with other treatments until it is healed.[I have translated this text based on the context provided and my knowledge of Old French medical terminology. However, please note that translations from medieval texts can be challenging due to differences in language usage and medical knowledge between then and now. I hope this translation helps you gain a better understanding of the original text!]
of dropsy (edema) We have previously said in the division the similarities of dropsy, and how the gathering of water is made, and the signs of each similarity, and the cure that is done by medicines, and that which is cured by iron: only mentioned here; and one should not touch the iron in these two similarities: namely, tympanites and hydrops ascites, for it is a deadly thing; therefore when you treat this similarity of dropsy which is called "eclites" with medicines, and the cure does not benefit, look whether the patient is weak, or if he has another illness besides dropsy, such as consumption and loosening of the bowels and similar things; then do not touch it with iron, for there is doubt; and if you see the patient of strong vigor, and he has no other illness but dropsy alone, and he is neither too young nor too old, then you should proceed thus: Lift the patient upright in your hands, and have the assistant press the belly, pushing the water towards the lowest part of the bladder, then take the pointed spatula as follows: And it should be sharpened on both sides, but not so penetrating that it can reach the intestines when inserted, and cannot cut or injure (them);
then look to see if the dropsy has advanced towards the intestines: if so, you must make an incision of three fingers' breadth above the navel, right under the belly button; and if the dropsy is due to liver disease, make your incision on the left side of the navel, three fingers' breadth; and if it is due to spleen disease, make it on the right, and be sure that the patient does not lie on the incision so that the superfluities do not run to this weak place;
then pierce the skin with the instrument, and push it, and lift your hand to the whole width of the spatula between the skin and the abscess, as when flaying to the length of a fingernail or thereabouts; then pierce the abscess until the spatula reaches the visible opening, and that is the location of the water, then pull it out and insert this instrument of which this is the shape: And it is similar to a thin silver cane, and is made of light iron or copper, and has in its lower part a small hole; and on one side, three holes; and two on the other, and one on the other; and the incision is cut like a pen nib, and this is the shape of the incision that the scribes cut the pen:
on top of the head is the collector, so that when the instrument reaches the water, it then descends onto the instrument; empty then half or a third of it, because if you empty more than necessary at that hour, perhaps the patient will die from the resolution of his spirit by the weapon, or he will fall unconscious, or he will approach death.
but empty it according to the quantity of its strength, and according to what the dispositions of the patient's strength indicate and his pulse; then remove the instrument and retain the water, which is now retained by the cork that stops the hole above the abscess, which I mentioned, that you pierce in this manner; then replace the instrument another day if you see the patient tolerating it, and draw a small quantity of water.
Do this for several days, and be careful not to leave too much water in it. And if you are unsure about the patient, leave a lot of water, then cure him with burial or hot sand, and cause him to sweat profusely or bathe in the sun, and dye him with drink; and cure him with drying medicines until he is healed, and cauterize on the stomach and on the liver and on the spleen after the extraction of the water, as I said before.
of the stopping of the penis Sometimes a child is born without an opening in the penis, or the opening is small, or it is not in its proper place. Then you must immediately make a tiny incision of about one spatulam (a unit of measurement) following this form: then insert a thin piece of linen cloth into the opening; bind and hold it in place for three or four days. When he wishes to urinate, remove the cloth and allow him to do so, and then replace the cloth; perhaps there will be no need, since the flow of urine will not close it. For those with a small opening, the treatment is to insert a piece of linen cloth as mentioned earlier, and it will widen. Those who do not have an opening in its proper place are wounded, for some are born with the opening at the tip of the penis, causing them to be unable to urinate unless they lift their penis upwards; and they cannot ejaculate because the matter cannot reach the glans, which is a bad disease.
And the method of this procedure is for the patient to lie down with his back towards you, then stretch out the head of the penis and hold it so that the opening faces directly upwards, as it should be; and take care to avoid any bleeding which occurs frequently, and if it does occur, use a styptic to stop the bleeding and treat the wound until it heals.
of pustules on the penis Often these ulcers appear on the penis, and it is an ugly appearance of flesh; and there are malignant and non-malignant ones; so one must carefully take them with a thin instrument, cut and clean all of them, then apply a cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment; then cure it with Diafinicon ointment until it is healed. And if the ulcer is malignant, of a putrid color and discharge, then cautery should be applied. When it has been cut out, and if the ulcer is in the middle of the penis, with some of it under the skin and some outside, then one must remove the part that is under the skin first; and when it has healed, then cure the outer part. For if it is cured while still attached, there is no certainty that the head of the penis will not be pierced, causing blackening and corruption on the penis and in the groin; one must remove all that is blackened and corrupted, then apply a plaster of honey and flour made from pomegranate rinds and vinegar, then cure it with other cures until it is healed.
And if bleeding occurs, then the lunar cautery should be applied in this manner: The cautery has two functions combined: that is to stop bleeding and to treat corruption; and if the head of the penis becomes corrupted and retracted, then a reed made of boxwood should be inserted into the penis so that the patient may urinate through it; and when the skin on the head of the penis (which happens in those who have not been circumcised) becomes wet due to wound or abscess, then one must use a small instrument to scrape it until the foreskin is loosened and the head of the penis is relieved all around.
And if it is a serious matter to discuss, you should scar it with a small spatula, either at the tip of the foreskin of the penis or the skin; then carefully separate it between two parts, and place a thin linen cloth soaked in cold water, but not wet again.
of circumcision
Circumcision is not a simple cutting of continuity like other wounds; nevertheless, when we perform this work of our own will, and especially in children, it is necessary that we do the best work, and the most gentle to heal.
I say that the ancients did not practice circumcision on their children, as they did not use it in their law, and we only have those who have been acquired through experience.
Because I have seen that the majority of masters and surgeons who perform circumcision around one day and a half, and with force, and using a novaculum; the cautery avoids it because there are two layers, and by chance, one may cut the upper layer and leave the lower one.
Or they cover it with a bandage, and the pain is doubled, and at the cleansing which is done on the day of the operation, the wound may not be secure; and during the cleansing of the nail by chance the iron may slip, and ruin the work, and many times it happens that there is no need for cleansing, and I have seen it.
In the cleansing which is done with force, I have found the advantage of the experiment, because cutting with force is as accurate above as below.
Because when you press your hand, you cut evenly in one steady motion; but the thread should be tightly wrapped around the skin; and the manner of the first operation is such that you make the child think, if they are aware, that you are only tying the thread around the penis, and then you delight them in all ways that you can. then place it upright in your hands, and apply pressure between your hands or under your feet so that the child cannot see them, nor any of the instruments; then place your hand on the swollen area and blow or cough, and pull upwards until the head of the swelling is outside; then clean it well of the dirt that has accumulated, then bind the wound with a double thread, then bind it underneath with one binding to another; and hold it with two fingers by the second binding at the thumb and index finger, and cut between the two bindings, then quickly lift the swollen area and pull the head of the swelling out.
then place it upright in your hands, and apply pressure between your hands or under your feet so that the child cannot see them, nor any of the instruments; then place your hand on the swollen area and blow or cough, and pull upwards until the head of the swelling is outside; then clean it well of the dirt that has accumulated, then bind the wound with a double thread, then bind it underneath with one binding to another; and hold it with two fingers by the second binding at the thumb and index finger, and cut between the two bindings, then quickly lift the swollen area and pull the head of the swelling out. then let the blood flow, for it is a purge; and put powder on it and on the powder a cloth glued with a half of a beaten egg mixed with rose-colored water, combined with good and fresh red oil, and leave it on until the next day, and cure it with other cures until it is healed.
The form of the forceps for cleaning is as follows: the two ends should be thin and sharp, and the ends should be as wide as a thumb equally.
The error in cleaning is when the core comes out at the incision; then you must stretch it to your fingernail so that the wound becomes an abscess; and cut it equally, and if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then hold it with pliers, and then cut it.
And if they pass three days, and what is beneath the groin is swollen, leave it until the abscess has calmed down, and gently flay it; cut it as movable as you can, and be careful near the groin, it will not harm him at all.
Then cure the wound of things that have stuck in it with powder, as we have said in the treatise on powders, and if you cut too deeply into the core and it goes beyond, it will not harm; so cure it, as we have said, until it is healed.
"of urinary retention"
"Urinary retention is caused by blockage from a stone, coagulated blood, tumor, or flesh-like substances, as we have said in the classification."
And if the urine is not released, and you see that its retention is due to a stone in the bladder or at the neck of the bladder, then the patient should bend over their knees; place their feet on their back, extend their belly on the side above the back, and restrain the patient's abdomen as much as they can; and then the neck of the bladder is constrained for the expulsion of the stone above, so that the urine is released.
And if it does not come out as we have said, and the patient feels great pain, then you should use the instrument called a "syringe" for extraction, which looks like this: made of thin and light silver, similar to bird feathers; and its cane should be one and a half palms long, and thin at the top.
And the way to release urine is to take a doubled thread and tie it to a piece of wool or cotton at its end, then insert the threaded end into the syringe; cut off excess wool and push it up to the top of the syringe, then anoint the syringe with oil or butter and egg yolk, make the patient sit on a seat, and apply moist oil, olive oil, and warm water to the bladder and genitals.
Then insert the syringe gently into the genitals until it reaches the base of the genitals, then bend the genitals upwards towards the belly; push the syringe forward to reach the bladder and make sure the patient feels the urine coming out through the empty vessel. This is not done in this order unless the conduit is twisted; then pull the thread along with all the wool piece, and the urine follows the wool; and then remove it outside and let the urine flow.
and refill the syringe until the bladder is emptied, and straighten out the patient.
diseases of the bladder
When ulcers or blood come into the bladder, or if pus has accumulated, and you wish to distill water and medicine into it, use an instrument called a "clepsydra," which is shaped as follows:
It is closed and has three openings, two on one side and one on the other, as you can see. The hairs on the end of the instrument that carries the medicine should be such that it stops up the urethra well without additional force, allowing for the removal of moisture and insertion when necessary.
And when you wish to introduce your medicines into the bladder, place the head of the instrument in the moisture and pull on the string that is attached. By doing so, you will draw the moisture into the instrument and then firmly push the string back in. The moisture will enter and reach the bladder, which the patient will feel.[1](footnote 1)
"and this is the form of the subtle enema with which to irrigate the bladder: it is as follows:"
"It is made of silver or brass, and the head is higher up; and below it has a bend where the liquid falls; then take a sheep's bladder and put the liquid you intend to irrigate the bladder with inside it, then tie it between the two breasts firmly with a good double thread, and heat one end of the liquid over the fire; then insert the head of the enema into the urethra, then squeeze your hand over the bladder until all the liquid has entered, so that the patient feels that the liquid has reached the bladder;"
"and if you don't have a bladder, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle out of it, then pierce a hole in the center; then insert a strong thread through this hole, then gather the circle as you would gather a bucket, and put inside it whatever liquid you want (oil, liquid, water), and tie it to the instrument; and do as you did with the bladder, squeezing your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder."
"from the stone"
We have previously said in the division the kinds of stones, and the extraction together with medicines; and I have mentioned the difference between the stone stuck in the kidneys, and all the signs, and in what kind of stone one should work by cutting; and in the bladder, and in the penis; and I will tell it to you clearly and concisely.
I say that the stone which is stuck in the bladder happens more often in children, and this is a sign that the water jumps from the bladder similarly to water in its subtlety; and sand appears, and the patient burns his penis, and urinates frequently, and then becomes rigid; and for many of these, the foundation is loose; and the healing is light in children until they reach 4 years old, and it is very painful in the elderly, and it is moderate in the young, for those who are between youth and old age; and the cure is easier for those who have large stones, than for those who have small ones.
And when it comes to the treatment, it is necessary to cleanse the patient first with an enema, to remove all the filth that is in the intestines. For it sometimes hinders the search for the stone.
Then take the patient by the feet, and stand them up and move them around, so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, and comes out somewhat; then make them sit between your hands prepared, and let their hands be beneath their thighs, so that the bladder is entirely inclined downwards; then search for it and touch it inside.
If you feel the stone in the area, hurry to cut into the stone. And if it does not fall at the slightest touch, moisten your thumb index finger with oil from your left hand, if the patient is elderly, or your middle finger if the patient is a fully grown young adult; and insert it into its foundation, and search for the stone, and when you feel it with your finger, pull on the stone at the neck of the bladder; then press with your finger, and push it out where you intend to cut.
And command the assistant to press the bladder with one hand, and order them to lift the hips with their right hand, and with their left hand remove the skin that is beneath the hips in the area where the incision is made; then insert the scalpel, which has the following shape:
And cut between the area that is between the hips and the perineum, not in the middle but to the side of the left testicle; and let it be straight on the stone, and your hands should be always at the perineum, and let the incision be wide from the outside, and narrow from the inside, according to the size of the stone, not too large.
It may happen that the pressure of the finger at the perineum will cause it to come out without difficulty; and know that there are some stones that have angles and sharp edges, which makes them difficult to pass; and there are some that are smooth, resembling a ball, and round, which makes it easier to extract.
To the one that has corners and edges, make the incision larger; and if it does not protrude, you should pull with pincers that are like a file at the top, to extract the stone so that it doesn't shatter; or you place underneath it an subtle curved instrument at the top; and if you can't deceive it in this way, widen the hole a little and if the flesh is cut, constrict it with vitriol.
And if there are multiple stones, place first the largest one at the neck of the bladder, then cut on top and pull it out, and the smaller ones afterwards if there are more. And if they are very large, they must be sawed and a larger incision must be made. For two things happen to the patient: either they die, or the urine comes continuously so that the wound cannot heal; but still try to pull or break it up with pincers until you pull it out in pieces.
And when you have completed your work, fill the wound with incense and aloe and dragon's blood, and bind it, and place on top a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in oil and rose water and cold water, to soothe the hot abscess; then turn the patient on their side, and do not loosen the binding until the third day.
And when you unbind it, rinse the wound well with oil and water, then cure it with a diaphoretic ointment and a basilicon ointment, until it is healed.[Additional requests:
And if abscesses or ulcers come in the wound and congealed blood in the bladder, and the urine is retained, which is known to happen without a sudden evacuation with the urine: then put your finger in the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains, it will corrupt the bladder and cause it to rot.
Then raise the wound of water and pus and corruption, and help it in all ways you know, until it is healed; and it is necessary that every time you treat it, you bind its two thighs together and apply the medicine to the affected area.
And if the stone is small and it is in the urethra and lodged in there, and the urine cannot come out; then heal it as I have said before, that you cut it many times, except for the cutting in this cure. I have proven this myself.[ANALYSIS]
Take a iron file of this shape and have three sharp points fixed in one handle:
then take a thread and tie it around the rod beneath the stone, so that it cannot slip back into the bladder; then gently insert the probe into the rod until it reaches the stone; and turn the probe with your hand in the stone repeatedly, as much as you can, until you can pierce the stone from the other side; soon urine will flow, then press your hand on the part that remains of the stone within the rod. It will break immediately and exit along with the urine, and the patient will be healed.
And if you cannot do that for some hindering factor, then tie a thread beneath the stone and another above it, then make an incision in the rod towards the stone between the two ties, then extract it outside and untie the tie, and clean the congealed blood that is in the wound; and cure it until it is healed.
of the female stone (i.e. kidney or bladder stones)
It rarely happens that a stone is inserted in women, and if it does occur, the cure is difficult and is hindered by three things: one is that perhaps the woman is a virgin; and the second is that one cannot find a skilled midwife who can examine her, even when she is chaste.
And the third manner is that perhaps one cannot find a woman skilled in this hand art; and the fourth manner is that the stone of the woman is deeper than that of the man, for which reason a deeper cut is needed, and there is great fear.
And if necessary, you must summon those (women) to you, then you should take a very skilled midwife, but they are hard to find, and if you cannot find one, then at least find a discreet female healer; and present her with a bowl of water and a wise woman in the affairs of women, or a woman who knows part of the art, and command her to do what you command regarding the examination of the stone.
And look very carefully if the woman is a virgin: then she must insert her finger into the vagina and search for the stone; and if she finds it, she should press it downwards beneath her finger, and command that she insert her finger into the womb of the patient, and press and hold her left hand firmly on the bladder.
If she finds it, then she must move it little by little from the entrance of the bladder down to the root of the thigh; then make an incision on the stone at the root of the thigh opposite the middle of the womb, where it is more prominent, and feel for the stone in that place; and do not move her finger above or below the stone, and let the first cut be small.
then put the knife in the incision, if you feel the stone, enlarge the wound so that the stone can exit, and know that there are many types of stones: that is to say, small and large, rough and smooth, long and round, and some have corners. So know their appearances to recognize the evacuation.
And if the blood overwhelms you, put vitriol powder on the wound, and hold it there for an hour until the bleeding stops; then return to your work, until the stone is out, and have the instruments with you that I mentioned in the extraction of the stone for men to help you in your work;
and if the blood overwhelms you, and it is an artery that is cut, put the powder on the wound and bind it with bandages and bandages, and do not start again, and leave the stone and do not pull it out, because perhaps the patient will die; then treat the wound.
And when the heat of the blood has calmed down and the wound dries, then return to your work, until the stone is out.[5] Then, once the stone is removed, you should cauterize the wound with fire to stop any further bleeding and promote healing. This can be done using a hot iron or other suitable cautery tool. It's important to ensure that the wound is clean and free of debris before cauterizing it, as any remaining particles or infection could lead to further complications.
of a carnal hernia
Ramice aquouse is nothing but a collection of moisture or white humor, which is under the skin of the groin, containing the egg; and it is called "syfac", and sometimes it is properly in the membrane of the egg, so that one might think it is another egg; and this is the skin of the groin that we mentioned earlier; and this only happens if there is a swelling.
And the formation of this ramice is due to weakness in the groin area, causing the spreading of that material.
And sometimes it occurs as a result of a blow to the groin, and this moisture has many colors: sometimes it turns yellowish, or is without redness, or black, or whitish-blue, and many other variations.
and these are the signs to recognize this abscess, for if it is white pus as we have said, the abscess is ripe, that is, a long swelling like the shape of an egg, and the corners do not appear, because it has moisture all around.
And if there is moisture in the proper membrane, the abscess is ripe with part of the egg, and for this reason, one thinks it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the corners and the white membrane, it can be felt well; therefore, when you want to know the color of the moisture, squeeze with a quadratic instrument, by which it is formed in front.[NOTE: I'm not sure what "quadratic instrument" means here. It may refer to some medical tool that was commonly used during that time. But based on the context, I assume that it refers to an instrument used for squeezing or extracting the moisture.] Then judge what is inside based on what comes after the instrument.[NOTE: This sentence means that one can determine the nature of the matter inside the abscess by observing the liquid or pus that comes out when the instrument is inserted and pressed against it.]

and if the kidney slips out of place during your work, put it back in its place when you have finished working on the kidney; then sew up the two leaves of the loins with one stitch, and treat it like other wounds until it is healed.

of the abscess of the hips
Know that in the sectioning of this vein, there is fear in many cases, for which reason it seems to me that it would be better to leave it alone.
and I will tell you the work and similarities of an abscess.
So I say that many abscesses come upon the body, even in the hips; and this is due to many reasons.
That is to say, by an acidic and sharp excess spread to the hips, or by percussion.
and is the color of the abscess similar to the color of the skin; and there is no finding of pain, and it is a painful abscess;
And perhaps it is an abscess, fearsome and hard, and of fuscous color, and sometimes comes from a ruptured artery, or swelling of a vein.
When it is due to a ruptured artery, it is known by the disposition of the abscess being hot when pressed with your hands. And one should not touch it at all.
The one who is made by swelling of the vein is known by its disposition on the expression of touching with the hands.
and one can easily perform surgery in a similar manner, and on other parts (of the body); and (you) should cut out the core of the kidneys, then pull up the ureter and remove the fatty tissue from it, and free the suspensory ligament from all sides of the ureter.

of the softening of the skin of the buttocks
Many times the skin of the buttocks is made soft in some men, because the fat that is inside is softened, and it is visible; and it is necessary, when one wants to cure it, to make the patient lie on their back, and cut the softened skin that is above the natural skin; then bring the two lips together with a suture, and if you see, first sew the excess softened skin with three or four stitches firmly; then cut between the stitches, and cure it like other wounds, until it is healed, and let the thread fall out.
of cautery/treatment. The word "chastrement" comes from the verb "chanter" which means "to burn." In medical contexts, it often refers to a treatment using fire or heat.
Know that cautery is recommended in our religion, and therefore I would not like to speak of it in this book; and it is only necessary for two reasons: one is so that the physician can respond knowledgeably when asked about it, and knows how to perform the treatment; and another reason is because we often need to use cautery on animals to help us, such as goats and sheep and pigs and other beasts. I therefore say that cautery is performed in two ways: either by attraction or by cutting. In the one done by attraction, we make the animal sit in hot water until the anus becomes soft and relaxed; then press with your hand until it returns to its normal position and you no longer feel it. Regarding the tail-cutting cautery: you must tie down the animal and press the skin of the anus with your left hand, then attach the suspensory ligament and cut on each side with one strike, after having properly skinned them; and leave some of the pouch of the scrotum and the fine scrotal tissue that is above the vessels; this method of cautery is more effective than the one done by attraction because there may be some remaining parts of the anus in the case of attraction, which makes the animal desire lust; then cure the wound until it is healed.
Hermaphrodites are individuals who possess both male and female sexual characteristics. The term comes from the Greek mythological figures Hermes and Aphrodite, whose child was born with both sets of genitalia. This condition can be caused by various factors, including genetic mutations or hormonal imbalances during fetal development. In medieval medicine, hermaphrodites were often discussed in works on sexual differentiation, abnormalities, and anomalies, as well as in legal texts related to gender identity and sexual behavior. The treatment of hermaphrodites varied depending on the cultural context and time period, but generally involved surgical intervention or hormonal therapy to assign a clear gender to the individual. However, it's important to note that these historical approaches were often based on limited scientific understanding and social biases, and do not necessarily reflect modern medical standards or ethical considerations.
Hermaphrodite is of two kinds in men: one kind is that between the two hips is open just like the shape of a woman's nature, where there is hair, and sometimes urine drips from it. And the other kind is in women, and they have something similar to a penis and two hips. And the treatment for both, man and woman, is to cut away the joined parts and cure them just as with other wounds. In the second kind of men, where urine leaps out through the skin of the hips, there is no cure.

Erratic is when the nature of the woman is not perceived, or the opening is small; and it is natural at birth, or accidental due to disease; or it is caused by a narrow cervix, or a subtle or thick syphilis, and is located deep in the womb, on the sides, or below, or elsewhere; and it prevents conception, childbirth, and possibly menstruation; and the recognition of this is through the examination of the vagina. If the blockages are apparent, seek with your fingers or a probe. If it is a subtle syphilis, and it is close to the two leaves, hurry and proceed as follows: place your two fingers together, and have the woman lie on her back, legs spread; then fully extend the two leaves so that the subtle syphilis is open, and lies over the blockages; then take wool, moisten it in oil, and place it on the cervix, and have the woman lie with a man each day, so that it does not close. And if the syphilis is hard and thick, then it is necessary to use a spatula-shaped instrument like a myrtle leaf. If the blockages are made of flesh, then lift it to the os pubis and cut; and have hemostatic medicines ready, such as alum, dragon's blood, incense, mixed with egg yolk; then prepare a wide horn cup, so that the wound does not close; and proceed in this manner for a long time, and apply a linen tent while it heals; and let it be treated with other cures until it is healed. And when flesh grows in the womb, it must be cut in this manner, unless it is a swelling that has grown. For a swelling that has grown in the womb, one should not touch it with iron.
For hemorrhoids:
Hemorrhoids are protrusions of veins that occur when they become swollen and enlarged, and when hemorrhoids become old, they can turn into thrombosed hemorrhoids. I have already mentioned their likenesses, signs, and now I will say those that receive treatment. I say that both hemorrhoids and thrombosed hemorrhoids, when they are inside the womb and not palpable, cannot be cured by surgery. Those that can be felt at the opening of the womb can be treated. It is necessary then to place the woman in a cool room, pull the hemorrhoid with pincers and cut it off at its roots, then apply hemostatic medicines such as alum and dragon's blood and incense, etc; then have her keep her feet against the wall for an hour, then put her in cold water; and if bleeding occurs, have her sit in a decoction of sumac and pomegranate bark and galls and similar things, and then make a flour of barley, mix it with honey and eggs, and apply it as a plaster on her back. And if they become dry, that is good. If not, apply cupping glasses to the back and breasts without breaking the skin; then take linen, moisten it in juice of plantain or cocklebur with old wine, and heat it at the location. When the swelling subsides, treat the location with an ointment until it is healed; and that which is called "albathir" is red and looks like a rough surface, it must be treated as I mentioned for thrombosed hemorrhoids, and then cure it until it is healed.
of abscesses on the groin
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the groin, such as chancre, abscess of the piles, herpes, estivation, fistula, hemorrhoids, hot abscess, and other illnesses that I have mentioned in the division. Therefore, it is appropriate in this treatise to discuss the hot abscess occurring in the groin, which is not one of those that burst by themselves; and if you wish to know about the operation with a tool, see if the abscess is hard at its beginning and full of heat and fever, and if it becomes red if you can see it. Then do not rush to pierce, but let it mature until the pain subsides; and then have the patient sit on a seat, and have two small seats for them to recline, and support their back and stretch out their legs past the thighs; and let their thighs be spread apart, and place their arms beneath their knees, and tie them well with a strong ligament; then turn them towards the right side, and prepare the instrument used to open the groin, of which follows the form; and it is necessary that when you wish to insert this instrument, you stick the probe first to know the depth of the abscess, so that what enters through the instrument does not go deeper than it should, and does not injure the patient; and if the instrument is larger, then it is necessary to cover the depth of the abscess with rags, so that the instrument does not harm it; and it is necessary to place the funnel of the instrument on top, and hold the instrument. And when the abscess opens and is pierced by hand, it must be covered with a cloth, at the tip of which there should be wool or lint; and once the pus has drained, wet bandages should be placed in the wound soaked in oil of roses or dry green oil; and place the bandage deep within the abscess and cover the groin and poignet with a clean linen cloth soaked in water or cooked herbs; then treat it after two days with appropriate ointment until it heals; and sometimes the groin and wound are cleared by an enema of water and honey, and then of water or roots of lilies and aristolochia longa that have been boiled; then return to the ointment. And if the wound is reopened in the groin, be careful not to burn with iron, but treat it as we have said in the division.
of childbirth
It is first necessary for the midwife to recognize the signs of natural childbirth, and one such sign is when you see the woman bending forward and craving to smell something; then the child hastens to come out; therefore know that this birth is in accordance with nature, and the baby comes out head first, followed by the afterbirth; and when you see these signs, then gently press on her stomach so that the baby comes out quickly; for when it settles on its head, the afterbirth follows with it, and the groin is cleared of its excess through good cleansing; and any birth that comes differently does not come naturally. For sometimes the baby comes feet first, or comes out body or limbs first, or hands, or comes out on its stomach, or turns around its neck, and in other bad ways; therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and clever in these things, and to be wary of error and deception, and I will show you all the signs and how one should help in each case.
If the child comes out of the womb headfirst, and the woman gives birth with difficulty, and her strength is weak, make her sit on a stool or have women hold her; and anoint her opening with oil or cooked fenugreek, and then place the small spatula (whose form is described here: ...) between her thighs. And split the membrane, and cut the fingernail down to where the moisture is; and support the woman's abdomen until the child descends, and if it does not descend, then you must give her an enema of muskillaige of fenugreek with oil of sesame; then make her drink a concoction and sneeze, and hold her mouth and nose for an hour: the child will now come out. And if both hands come out first, then you should gently push them back in one after the other; and if they cannot enter, then make the woman sit on a stool, and lift her legs up high; then lower the stool to the ground, and be sure she does not fall during this movement; and if the hands still cannot enter, and the child is dead, then cut off its hands with the knife that is placed here, and extract the child, and reset it as well.

The earliest recorded use of cautery dates back to the ancient Egyptians, who used it for various purposes such as treating wounds and stopping bleeding. This method was also widely used in ancient Greece, where Hippocrates, the father of medicine, described its use in his works. Even today, cauterization is still used in modern medicine, although it has been largely replaced by more advanced techniques.

of wounds
We have previously said in the division the treatment of wounds; now we will tell you the treatments for hands and remedies, according to the completion.
I say that wounds are diverse according to how they are made, and according to the places where the wounds are made, and by what they are made.
The things by which wounds are made are by the striking of a stone, cutting with a sword and knife, or a lance or stick or arrow, or similar things.
Or wounds are made according to the parts of the body, such as a wound that falls on the neck, or the head, or the chest, or the stomach, or the liver, and similar members.[ST1] ;

The rupture (or tear) of the belly is large, medium, and small;
During a large rupture (or tear) of the belly, sometimes the intestines or the gut membranes protrude, causing an intrusion (or invasion).[JM1]
and the suturing (or stitching) is more difficult, and sometimes a small wound is worse than a large one.
Because one puts back the intestines with points [stitches], if they are not put back hot on hot; Because the intestines swell as soon as they feel cold, and thus they return more easily with stitches.
And it is better to move the middle wound because one can reset the intestines in its place more easily than the other two.\*

of the shoulder fracture/dislocation
It rarely happens that the shoulder breaks or dislocates in a large or small place, and it is not noticed until touched; then set it according to the type of fracture, and reset it according to its natural shape, in all ways possible; then put on the surface powder of flour with plenty of egg, and light cotton, and place a double cloth over it; then place a wide and thin plate over it, a little wider than the shoulder, and if there are any hairs under the plate in any part of the shoulder, fill those hairs with linen cotton, so that the plate lies flat; and then bind it tightly with a large bandage, making sure that the plate does not move from its place, and check the binding every day; and whenever it softens, tighten it again, and make sure the plate is in place if it has moved, and have the patient lie on his side; and the shoulder will be healed in 20 or 25 days. And when this number of days is completed, unbind it and be careful, for it is not an easy bone to put back in place.
And if a piece of bone sticks out in front and not under the skin, then saw through it (the bone), and remove it, and do what we have said for the fracture of the forearm to soothe the hot abscess.[Notes: I am assuming "apaiser aposteme chaut" means to treat a hot abscess, as it is mentioned before in the text. The word "oste" here probably means to saw off the protruding bone. ]
of the break of the foot
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, but this happens rarely; however, it often breaks at the ankle. And when it is broken in the middle, it tilts downwards, and the patient feels great pain, and there is a lack of alignment, and they may vomit blood, and the fracture can be seen in the broken bone, and it is evident to others.
And the restoration is such that the patient lies down with their back on a cushion placed between their shoulders, then presses their shoulders and brings the ribs together using both hands, subtly aligning all parts of the bone as much as possible, until the shape of the bone is as it should be; then apply poultices and bandages over the fracture, with a thin layer of salt or other light wood, and wrap it in a cloth, tying it securely to the broken bone so that it does not move; tie the knot around their back, pulling it tight, and check every hour to see if it needs to be loosened, due to infection or itching at the site, or pain or abscess, and act quickly by untying it, removing the poultice, addressing any issues that have arisen through appropriate treatments; then reapply the poultice, tying it securely until healed.
of the broken ribs
Know that the breakers of ribs do not come about except in thick areas after the back, and at the head within, and they do not bend outwards except because they are slippery; and one can feel this well upon examination at the sides.
And the setting (of a broken bone) is such that you make the break even, as it should be. And then apply an ointment and bind it with a splint if necessary.
And if the break of the ribs is declining inwards, then the patient feels great pain and swelling, just like with pleurisy, because the bone points at the membrane; and there is sharp pain, and soon they vomit a large amount of blood, and this is of grave illness, and the old ones have applied much skill to it.
And there are some who say that one should administer foods that inflate the belly, in order to push the break outwards.
We do not make it so that boils/abscesses come to it, if they have not already come. And if they have come, then lance them (let the pus out).
And some of you have said that cupping glasses should be placed over the place to strongly draw out (suction), and this seems reasonable; however, there is doubt that the cupping glass might attract impurities to the place due to its weak constitution.
And some of you have said that one should cover the place with a cloth soaked in warm oil and then place bandages between the ribs to evenly hold it in place around the area; and then treat it with the treatment for pleurisy, food, and medicine.
And if the patient feels great pain that they cannot bear from the bone pressing against the cloth, and there is doubt for the patient, then one must make an incision on the place of the broken rib, and place a dressing underneath to protect the wound; and then cut the bone and gently pull it out, and then sew up the two lips of the wound if it is large, and dress it with poultices until it heals. And if boils/abscesses come, then place cloths soaked in rose oil on the area, and place them on the place; and treat the area as one would for a boil, and let the patient lie on their side where the wound is, until it heals.
of the break (fracture) of the neck
Never does a break occur in the bones of the neck, and it rarely happens that it bends; and likewise for the spine of the back. And when this happens to someone, and you want to know if they will be healed or not, pay attention if you see their hands softened, swollen, and numb, and they cannot move or extend them, and when you poke them with a needle, they do not feel it. Know that in many cases, this means they are mortally injured, but if they can move them and feel when they are squeezed and poked, know that the joint of the bone is saved, and that the patient will be cured if given treatment.
And if similar things happen to the spine of the back, and you want to know if they will be healed or not, pay attention if their feet are weakened, and if the conditions present in their hands are also there, and on their back, a wind-like movement and involuntary excretions occur; and when it is placed on its stomach, involuntary excretions still occur. And when it is placed on its back and it wants to sit up, it cannot. Know that this is mortal: do not labor in its cure.
And if these conditions do not arise, then the matter is more manageable.
And the treatment is that you should check if there is a hot abscess, and if there is, put roset oil or egg yolk oil on it until it calms down; then apply one of the soothing and drying plasters, and bind it with a bandage, and instruct the patient to rest; and let them not sleep on the part where they feel pain, until it is healed. And if any bone becomes dislocated, it is necessary to cover the wound; remove the bone, then bring together the two edges of the large wound with a stitch, and treat it with a drawing ointment until it heals; and if the joint near the bone is broken, which is called "the hip joint," place your left hand's pouch on it, and use your right hand to push it into place as much as you can, then apply the plaster and bandage if needed; then bind it, and if there is a dislocated bone, push it back into place, remove it, and treat the wound until it heals.
of the fracture of the femur (thigh) bone.
It rarely happens that the femur is broken, but if it does happen, only a slight swelling occurs at the end; and it becomes long, and inclines to one side, causing pain and lameness to the patient in that area, and swelling in the affected limb.
And the restoration is as follows: you extend your hand on top, so that you are above the break; and know what the break and its shape is; and if the break is at the head only, then make the break even, so that it is in its natural shape.
And if the break is long, or it angles inward, then have the patient lie on their stomach, so that the restoration of that break is made clear to you. And when you have made it even, then apply the ointment, then place a wooden or waxen star* on top, and hold the break steady so that it does not move; and apply medicine underneath the ribs, until they are aligned.[Yves57] *It is likely that "estele" here refers to some kind of medical device made of wood or wax. The meaning of this term in Old French is uncertain and could also refer to a type of bandage or splint.
And command the patient to sleep on their healthy side, or on their back.
And if abscesses come after it, do not touch it until the abscesses have calmed down, and apply what we have said before; and then return to the restoration, and bind it as is necessary, if there is no adverse accident; and this is the method.
of the shoulder dislocation.
The shoulder joint is the one that is between the neck and the shoulder blade, and if it is dislocated, it should be restored in one of two ways: one of which is to take a rod of medium thickness, according to this shape:
And tie the two shoulders with two bands and pull them up, and make the patient sit on a seat; then throw his dislocated arm over the rod, until it reaches the waist, and has in the middle a bow of the rod; then hang it from some heavy weight or have the minister stretch it down; then place the surgeon's hands equally around the break, until the break returns to its proper position.
And the other manner is that one lays the patient on their back, and ties their hand to their neck, then commands two assistants, such that one holds what is above the break with their hands, and the other holds beneath all the way to one ligament, and each pulls apart in their direction.
And if you see that the extraction is too strong, then one assistant pulls a ligament below the break and above it, while each of the assistants pulls apart in their direction. And if the break is close to the shoulder joint, then one must place a middle ligament below the socket and another towards the ribcage, below the break; and similarly if the break is towards the ribcage, one must place a light ligament without strain so that the break is even above the socket and above the ribcage; then align the break as it should be and hold it in place, if no abscess forms.
And if an abscess does form, then do not hold it in place for more than 7 days and place a moist linen cloth soaked in rose oil on the affected area; wait until the abscess subsides. Then hold it in place, and the manner of holding it in place is that you apply a poultice to the break, then position the arm on the shoulder, and place your hand on the shoulder or chest; and place the cloth, and hold it in place on the shoulder and on the arm so that the arm remains still, unless something prevents you, or if the bone has not moved.
And if you are unsure whether the bone has moved, then use a splint and place a wider and stronger splint over the break than the others, with a space of one finger between each splint, and let each splint be large enough to extend beyond the break by three fingers on either side; then hold it in place over the splints as I mentioned at the beginning of this chapter, more firmly and less so as you move away.
And if you see the swelling and tightening, as we have mentioned, at the time you reset the broken member, and you doubt there is a hot abscess, then leave it to bind until the seventh day, as we have said; then examine the binding every three days, to see if itching, inflammation or discharge comes to the place, or if numbness cannot reach the member because the binding is too tight. Loosen it, as we have said.
And if no such accidents occur, then do not loosen it before three days or more, and let the patient sleep on their back, with their hands on their stomach; and place a cushion full of evenly spread wool beneath the affected area, and examine every hour of the day and night, to see if the shape of the broken member moves, or if the binding becomes too loose; then adjust it by the power of medicine, and give them food as we have previously mentioned, in such a way that the food is initially fine; and when they begin to recover, let the food be coarse. And it is customary for the affected area and leg to fully recover in forty days; and then one should loosen it and administer baths and medicines with ointments to straighten the place. And if the stricture is bad and embedded, then wait one day or two months before loosening it.
Here's the translation of the text you provided, in relation to a broken arm:
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the "foscile", one of which is small and is the one that follows the wrist; and the other is large, and it is the one that is beneath, towards the little finger; by chance, only the large foscile breaks, or the small one, and by chance, both together.
When the small bones are broken, the restoration and healing is easy; and when the large ones are broken, the healing is difficult and more severe than when they are both broken together. If it is only the small bone that is broken, then it should be gently stretched and put in its place.
And if it is the large bone, then it must be strained more; and it should be stretched out on a cushion, and the little finger of the hand should be held higher than the others, and the small toes lower than the other toes, and the patient should be lying crosswise on it, with a cushion before him that is higher than he can bear. Then the assistants should extend the patient's arms below to his side, or to a strap, and one assistant at one side and another at the other. And then the physician puts it in its place, according to its best form.
And if the break is in pieces, then put each one in its place; and if there is any piece that pricks the heart, and you cannot restore it to its place, then make an incision above it and remove it, as we have said before.
And if there is a wound with the break, I have spoken of this in a separate chapter; so take care of it (referring to the treatment described in the previous chapter).
And if in the beginning of the restoration pustules occur, then put on a piece of linen cloth dipped in a mixture of rose oil and white wax, neither too hard nor too soft; and carefully place another cloth over it, until the pustules have calmed down. Then remove the linen cloth and apply the plaster made of powder from the mill with plenty of egg, then put on the stylets, and let the one from the breakage be stronger and a little wider than the others; and know that it should have six stylets or branches in multiple arrangements: either the breakage be in one of the bones, or in two; and bind more above the breakage than any other part, and always less as you move away, as I said at the beginning of this chapter; and let the cloths that are above the breakage be soft and moist, and not hard or rough; and let the thread from which it is bound be of linen, neither too thick nor too thin, as we have said. And wrap the member and the bandage after some days. And if some misfortune befalls the member, such as itching that comes to the member, then you must sprinkle warm water on the member until the itching has calmed down; and let the member not rest tightly. And then begin binding again, and if the bandages are weakened and the bones have separated, and similar things, address them.
And if you notice that the circulation cannot reach the member because it is too tight, then you must soften it a little and leave it like that for several days, until the circulation has returned, and then bind it again.
And if none of the things that we have mentioned happen to the patient, then do not unbind it for twenty days or so; and in the meantime, let him keep his hands on his neck, with his collars even, and beware of movement and rubbing, and let him sleep on his back. And know that this breakage is healed in thirty or thirty-five days, and perhaps even in twenty days, depending on the compositions and complexions of the humors.
